				<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body" aria-labelledby="firstHeading" data-mw-ve-target-container>
					<div class="vector-body-before-content">
							<div class="mw-indicators">
		</div>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
					</div>
					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about sets of vertices connected by edges. For graphs of mathematical functions, see <a href="/wiki/Graph_of_a_function" title="Graph of a function">Graph of a function</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Graph_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Graph (disambiguation)">Graph (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Area of discrete mathematics</div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Example_of_simple_undirected_graph_3.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Example_of_simple_undirected_graph_3.svg/250px-Example_of_simple_undirected_graph_3.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="132" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Example_of_simple_undirected_graph_3.svg/500px-Example_of_simple_undirected_graph_3.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="97" data-file-height="64" /></a><figcaption>A graph with 6 vertices and 7 edges</figcaption></figure>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>, <b>graph theory</b> is the study of <a href="/wiki/Graph_(discrete_mathematics)" title="Graph (discrete mathematics)">graphs</a>, which are <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_structures" class="mw-redirect" title="Mathematical structures">mathematical structures</a> used to model pairwise relations between objects. A graph in this context is made up of <a href="/wiki/Vertex_(graph_theory)" title="Vertex (graph theory)">vertices</a> (also called nodes or points) which are connected by <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_graph_theory_terms#edge" class="mw-redirect" title="Glossary of graph theory terms">edges</a> (also called arcs, links, or lines). A distinction is made between undirected graphs, where edges link two vertices symmetrically, and <a href="/wiki/Directed_graph" title="Directed graph">directed graphs</a>, where edges link two vertices asymmetrically. Graphs are one of the principal objects of study in <a href="/wiki/Discrete_mathematics" title="Discrete mathematics">discrete mathematics</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Definition_and_etymology">Definition and etymology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Graph_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition and etymology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Graph_(discrete_mathematics)" title="Graph (discrete mathematics)">Graph (discrete mathematics)</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Undirected,_directed,_mixed,_multigraph.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Undirected%2C_directed%2C_mixed%2C_multigraph.svg/250px-Undirected%2C_directed%2C_mixed%2C_multigraph.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="250" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Undirected%2C_directed%2C_mixed%2C_multigraph.svg/500px-Undirected%2C_directed%2C_mixed%2C_multigraph.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="283" data-file-height="283" /></a><figcaption>A graph consists of vertices connected by edges. A graph is occasionally called:<p class="mw-empty-elt"></p> <div><ul><li>An undirected graph (top left), distinguishing it from a <a href="/wiki/Directed_graph" title="Directed graph">directed graph</a> that has an arrow on each edge (top right). Undirected and directed graphs can be merged into a <a href="/wiki/Mixed_graph" title="Mixed graph">mixed graph</a> (bottom left); and</li><li>A simple graph, distinguishing it from a <a href="/wiki/Multigraph" title="Multigraph">multigraph</a> (bottom right).</li></ul></div> </figcaption></figure>
<p>Graph theory is a branch of mathematics that studies <a href="/wiki/Graph_(discrete_mathematics)" title="Graph (discrete mathematics)">graphs</a>, a mathematical structure for modelling pairwise relations between objects. The term "graph" was introduced by <a href="/wiki/James_Joseph_Sylvester" title="James Joseph Sylvester">James Joseph Sylvester</a> in a paper published in 1878 in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i>, where he drew an analogy between "quantic invariants" and "co-variants" of algebra and molecular diagrams.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESylvester1878&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESylvester1878-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The definition of a graph can vary, but one can understand that a graph is a structure that contains the vertices (also called nodes or points) and edges (also called arcs, links, or lines). Two vertices of an edge are called the endpoints.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEOre1962&#91;httpsarchiveorgdetailstheoryofgraphs0000oreypage1&#95;1&#93;&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOre1962[httpsarchiveorgdetailstheoryofgraphs0000oreypage1_1]-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Occasionally, a graph is called an undirected graph, to distinguish it from a <a href="/wiki/Directed_graph" title="Directed graph">directed graph</a>. A directed graph is a graph where each edge has an assignment direction known as <a href="/wiki/Orientation_(graph_theory)" title="Orientation (graph theory)">orientation</a>, designated with an arrow.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEOre1962&#91;httpsarchiveorgdetailstheoryofgraphs0000oreypage2&#95;2&#93;&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOre1962[httpsarchiveorgdetailstheoryofgraphs0000oreypage2_2]-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Mixed_graph" title="Mixed graph">mixed graph</a> can have edges that may be directed, and some may be undirected.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEOre1962&#91;httpsarchiveorgdetailstheoryofgraphs0000oreypage3&#95;3&#93;&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOre1962[httpsarchiveorgdetailstheoryofgraphs0000oreypage3_3]-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A graph can also be called a simple graph, to distinguish it from a <a href="/wiki/Multigraph" title="Multigraph">multigraph</a>. A multigraph allows many edges to have the same pair of endpoints, and it also allows an edge connect a vertex to itself, known as a <a href="/wiki/Loop_(graph_theory)" title="Loop (graph theory)">loop</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBollobas2013&#91;httpbooksgooglecombooksidJeIlBQAAQBAJpgPA7&#95;7&#93;&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBollobas2013[httpbooksgooglecombooksidJeIlBQAAQBAJpgPA7_7]-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A graph can have its edges assigned a number, which is known as the weight. Such a graph is called a weight graph.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Graph_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Konigsberg_bridges.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Konigsberg_bridges.png/250px-Konigsberg_bridges.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="197" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5d/Konigsberg_bridges.png 2x" data-file-width="302" data-file-height="238" /></a><figcaption>Map of <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6nigsberg" title="Königsberg">Königsberg</a> from 1651 showing the layout of the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Bridges_of_K%C3%B6nigsberg" title="Seven Bridges of Königsberg">seven bridges</a>, highlighting the <a href="/wiki/Pregel_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Pregel River">Pregel River</a> (in blue) and the bridges (in lime). The problem lays the foundation of both graph theory and <a href="/wiki/Topology" title="Topology">topology</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>In 1736, <a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a> published a paper titled <i>Solutio Problematis ad Geometriam Situs Pertinentis</i> on the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Bridges_of_K%C3%B6nigsberg" title="Seven Bridges of Königsberg">Seven Bridges of Königsberg</a>, which is regarded as the first paper in the history of graph theory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBiggsLloydWilson1986&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidXqYTk0sXmpoCpgPA2&#95;2–3&#93;&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiggsLloydWilson1986[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidXqYTk0sXmpoCpgPA2_2–3]-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Euler's paper and <a href="/wiki/Alexandre-Th%C3%A9ophile_Vandermonde" title="Alexandre-Théophile Vandermonde">Alexandre-Théophile Vandermonde</a>'s 1771 <i>Remarques sur les Problèmes de Situation</i> paper on the <a href="/wiki/Knight%27s_tour" title="Knight&#39;s tour">knight's tour</a> carried on with the <i>analysis situs</i>, initiated by <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBiggsLloydWilson1986&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidXqYTk0sXmpoCpgPA21&#95;21–22&#93;&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiggsLloydWilson1986[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidXqYTk0sXmpoCpgPA21_21–22]-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Euler%27s_characteristic" class="mw-redirect" title="Euler&#39;s characteristic">Euler's characteristic</a> relating the number of edges, vertices, and faces of a convex polyhedron was studied and generalized by <a href="/wiki/Augustin-Louis_Cauchy" title="Augustin-Louis Cauchy">Augustin-Louis Cauchy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simon_Antoine_Jean_L%27Huilier" title="Simon Antoine Jean L&#39;Huilier">Simon Antoine Jean L'Huilier</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and represents the beginning of the branch of mathematics known as <a href="/wiki/Topology" title="Topology">topology</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTERicheson200863&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERicheson200863-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>More than one century after Euler's paper on the bridges of <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6nigsberg" title="Königsberg">Königsberg</a>, and while <a href="/wiki/Johann_Benedict_Listing" title="Johann Benedict Listing">Johann Benedict Listing</a> was introducing the concept of topology, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Cayley" title="Arthur Cayley">Arthur Cayley</a> was led by an interest in particular analytical forms arising from <a href="/wiki/Differential_calculus" title="Differential calculus">differential calculus</a> to study a particular class of graphs, the <a href="/wiki/Tree_(graph_theory)" title="Tree (graph theory)">trees</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECayley1875&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECayley1875-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This study had many implications for theoretical <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>. The techniques he used mainly concern the <a href="/wiki/Enumeration_of_graphs" class="mw-redirect" title="Enumeration of graphs">enumeration of graphs</a> with particular properties. Enumerative graph theory then arose from the results of Cayley and the fundamental results published by <a href="/wiki/George_P%C3%B3lya" title="George Pólya">Pólya</a> between 1935 and 1937. These were generalized by <a href="/wiki/Nicolaas_Govert_de_Bruijn" title="Nicolaas Govert de Bruijn">Nicolaas Govert de Bruijn</a> in 1959. Cayley linked his results on trees with contemporary studies of chemical composition.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTECayley1875&#95;10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECayley1875-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The fusion of ideas from mathematics with those from chemistry began what has become part of the standard terminology of graph theory.
</p><p>The autonomous development of topology from 1860 to 1930 fertilized graph theory back through the works of <a href="/wiki/Camille_Jordan" title="Camille Jordan">Camille Jordan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazimierz_Kuratowski" title="Kazimierz Kuratowski">Kazimierz Kuratowski</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hassler_Whitney" title="Hassler Whitney">Hassler Whitney</a>. Another important factor in the common development of graph theory and topology came from the use of the techniques of modern algebra. The first example of such a use comes from the work of the physicist <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Kirchhoff" title="Gustav Kirchhoff">Gustav Kirchhoff</a>, who published in 1845 his <a href="/wiki/Kirchhoff%27s_circuit_laws" title="Kirchhoff&#39;s circuit laws">Kirchhoff's circuit laws</a> for calculating the <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">voltage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">current</a> in <a href="/wiki/Electric_circuit" class="mw-redirect" title="Electric circuit">electric circuits</a>.
</p><p>The first textbook on graph theory was written by <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A9nes_K%C5%91nig" title="Dénes Kőnig">Dénes Kőnig</a>, and published in 1936.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTETutte2001&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksiduTGhooU37h4CpgPA30&#95;30&#93;&#95;11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETutte2001[httpsbooksgooglecombooksiduTGhooU37h4CpgPA30_30]-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another book by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Harary" title="Frank Harary">Frank Harary</a>, published in 1969, was "considered the world over to be the definitive textbook on the subject",<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGardner1992203&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGardner1992203-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and enabled mathematicians, chemists, electrical engineers and social scientists to talk to each other. Harary donated all of the royalties to fund the <a href="/wiki/George_P%C3%B3lya_Prize" title="George Pólya Prize">Pólya Prize</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESociety&#95;for&#95;Industrial&#95;and&#95;Applied&#95;Mathematics200226&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESociety_for_Industrial_and_Applied_Mathematics200226-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>One of the most famous problems in graph theory is the <a href="/wiki/Four_color_problem" class="mw-redirect" title="Four color problem">four color problem</a>: Is it true that any map drawn in the plane may have its regions colored with four colors, in such a way that any two regions having a common border have different colors? This problem was first posed by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Guthrie" title="Francis Guthrie">Francis Guthrie</a> in 1852, and its first written record is in a letter of <a href="/wiki/Augustus_De_Morgan" title="Augustus De Morgan">Augustus De Morgan</a> addressed to <a href="/wiki/William_Rowan_Hamilton" title="William Rowan Hamilton">William Rowan Hamilton</a> the same year. Many incorrect proofs have been proposed, including those by Augustin Cayley, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Kempe" title="Alfred Kempe">Alfred Kempe</a>, and others. The study and the generalization of this problem by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Tait_(physicist)" class="mw-redirect" title="Peter Tait (physicist)">Peter Tait</a>, <a href="/wiki/Percy_John_Heawood" title="Percy John Heawood">Percy John Heawood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frank_P._Ramsey" title="Frank P. Ramsey">Frank P. Ramsey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Hadwiger" title="Hugo Hadwiger">Hadwiger</a> led to the study of the colorings of the graphs embedded on surfaces with arbitrary <a href="/wiki/Genus_(mathematics)" title="Genus (mathematics)">genus</a>. Tait's reformulation generated a new class of problems, the <i>factorization problems</i>, particularly studied by <a href="/wiki/Julius_Petersen" title="Julius Petersen">Petersen</a> and <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A9nes_K%C5%91nig" title="Dénes Kőnig">Dénes Kőnig</a>. The works of Ramsey on colorations, and more specially, the results obtained by <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A1l_Tur%C3%A1n" title="Pál Turán">Pál Turán</a> in 1941, were at the origin of another branch of graph theory, known as <a href="/wiki/Extremal_graph_theory" title="Extremal graph theory">extremal graph theory</a>.
</p><p>The four-color problem remained unsolved for more than a century. In 1969, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Heesch" title="Heinrich Heesch">Heinrich Heesch</a> published a method for solving the problem using computers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A computer-aided proof produced in 1976 by <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Appel" title="Kenneth Appel">Kenneth Appel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Haken" title="Wolfgang Haken">Wolfgang Haken</a> makes fundamental use of the notion of "discharging" developed by Heesch.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-AA1&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AA1-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-AA2&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AA2-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The proof involved checking the properties of 1,936 configurations by computer, and was not fully accepted at the time due to its complexity. A simpler proof considering only 633 configurations was given twenty years later by <a href="/wiki/Neil_Robertson_(mathematician)" title="Neil Robertson (mathematician)">Robertson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Seymour_(mathematician)" title="Paul Seymour (mathematician)">Seymour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_P._Sanders" title="Daniel P. Sanders">Sanders</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robin_Thomas_(mathematician)" title="Robin Thomas (mathematician)">Thomas</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-RSST&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSST-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The introduction of <a href="/wiki/Probabilistic_method" title="Probabilistic method">probabilistic methods</a> in graph theory, especially in the study of <a href="/wiki/Paul_Erd%C5%91s" title="Paul Erdős">Erdős</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alfr%C3%A9d_R%C3%A9nyi" title="Alfréd Rényi">Rényi</a> of the asymptotic probability of graph connectivity, gave rise to yet another branch, known as <a href="/wiki/Random_graph" title="Random graph">random graph theory</a>, which has been a fruitful source of graph-theoretic results.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBollobás2001&#91;httpbooksgooglecombooksido9WecWgilzYCpgPR10&#95;xi&#93;&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBollobás2001[httpbooksgooglecombooksido9WecWgilzYCpgPR10_xi]-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Subareas">Subareas</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Graph_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Subareas"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Topological_graph_theory">Topological graph theory</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Graph_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Topological graph theory"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Topological_graph_theory" title="Topological graph theory">Topological graph theory</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Topological_graph_theory" title="Category:Topological graph theory">Category:Topological graph theory</a></div>

data-file-height="349" /></a></span></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Topological graph theory deals with the study of graphs related to topology. The topics, along with the given top-to-bottom illustrations, are
<div><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Graph_embedding" title="Graph embedding">Embedding of a graph</a> in a surface, with an example of <a href="/wiki/Heawood_graph" title="Heawood graph">Heawood graph</a> in torus;</li><li><a href="/wiki/Book_embedding" title="Book embedding">1-page embedding</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Diamond_graph" title="Diamond graph">diamond graph</a>;</li><li><a href="/wiki/Crossing_number_(graph_theory)" title="Crossing number (graph theory)">Crossing number</a>, with an example of a <a href="/wiki/Complete_bipartite_graph" title="Complete bipartite graph">complete bipartite graph</a> of four and seven vertices <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle K_{4,7}}">
  <semantics>

          <mi>K</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

            <mn>7</mn>

        </msub>
      </mstyle>

</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fb5a257465cad23b7b349e4f2974c03ae3446cd8" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:4.307ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle K_{4,7}}"></span>, having with 18 crossings (in red dots); and</li><li>Map coloring, using <a href="/wiki/Four-color_theorem" class="mw-redirect" title="Four-color theorem">four-color theorem</a> to color differently each <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">state</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, ignoring lakes and oceans.</li></ul></div></div></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Topological_graph_theory" title="Topological graph theory">Topological graph theory</a> deals with the study of graphs as <a href="/wiki/Topological_space" title="Topological space">topological spaces</a>. The graph in a topology is a set of <a href="/wiki/Simplex" title="Simplex">simplexes</a> that is called the <a href="/wiki/Simplicial_complex" title="Simplicial complex">simplicial one-dimensional complex</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGrossTucker2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid6HmA&#95;x0dL9oCpgPA1&#95;1&#93;&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrossTucker2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid6HmA_x0dL9oCpgPA1_1]-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This subarea studies the <a href="/wiki/Graph_embedding" title="Graph embedding">embedding</a> (or imbedding) of a graph in <a href="/wiki/Surface_(topology)" title="Surface (topology)">surface</a> and <a href="/wiki/Linkless_embedding" title="Linkless embedding">linkless embedding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Graph_minor" title="Graph minor">graph minors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crossing_number_(graph_theory)" title="Crossing number (graph theory)">crossing number</a>, map coloring, and <a href="/wiki/Voltage_graph" title="Voltage graph">voltage graph</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The embedding of a graph in a surface is the representation of a graph in which the points are associated with the vertices and simple arcs with edges in a surface. The endpoints are associated with an edge, and the points with the end vertices. No arcs include points associated with other vertices, and two arcs never intersect at a point that is interior to either of the arcs.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2026)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The graph embedding can be generalized into the linkless embedding, whereby no two cycles of the graph are linked in three-dimensional Euclidean space,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTELovász200676&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELovász200676-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Book_embedding" title="Book embedding">book</a>, a collection of <a href="/wiki/Half-space_(geometry)" title="Half-space (geometry)">half-planes</a> all having the same line as their boundary.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPersinger1966&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPersinger1966-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The graph is said to be minor if it can be formed from another graph by deleting vertices and edges, and by <a href="/wiki/Edge_contraction" title="Edge contraction">edge-contraction</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTELovász200676&#95;21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELovász200676-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The earliest result of the graph minor theory is from <a href="/wiki/Wagner%27s_theorem" title="Wagner&#39;s theorem">Wagner's theorem</a>, stating that a finite graph is planar if and only if its minor includes neither the <a href="/wiki/Complete_graph" title="Complete graph">complete graph on five vertices</a> <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle K_{5}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <msub>
          <mi>K</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>5</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle K_{5}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/10a83da34fe45aa3be9a7d0b197417021bb4a884" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.027ex; height:2.509ex;" alt="{\displaystyle K_{5}}"></span> nor the <a href="/wiki/Utility_graph" class="mw-redirect" title="Utility graph">utility graph</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTELovász200677&#95;23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELovász200677-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A related result is the <a href="/wiki/Robertson%E2%80%93Seymour_theorem" title="Robertson–Seymour theorem">Robertson–Seymour theorem</a>, implying the existence of <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Forbidden minor">forbidden minor</a> for every property of graphs preserved by deletions and edge contractions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTELovász200678Theorem&#95;4&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELovász200678Theorem_4-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The crossing number tells the minimum number of crossing edges of a graph. This study originated from a Hungarian mathematician <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A1l_Tur%C3%A1n" title="Pál Turán">Pál Turán</a> who <a href="/wiki/Tur%C3%A1n%27s_brick_factory_problem" title="Turán&#39;s brick factory problem">asked for a factory plan that minimized the number of crossings between tracks connecting brick kilns to storage sites</a>. This problem can be formalized as asking for the crossing number of a <a href="/wiki/Complete_bipartite_graph" title="Complete bipartite graph">complete bipartite graph</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEFoulds1992&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5G4QBwAAQBAJpgPA71&#95;71&#93;&#95;25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFoulds1992[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5G4QBwAAQBAJpgPA71_71]-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Graph_coloring" title="Graph coloring">graph coloring</a> is a methodical assignment of labelling the elements of a graph, which is traditionally called <a href="/wiki/Color" title="Color">colors</a>. In coloring, no two adjacent elements have the same color. It requires the minimum number of colors, which is known as the chromatic number. <a href="/wiki/Four-color_theorem" class="mw-redirect" title="Four-color theorem">Four-color theorem</a> stated that no more than four colors are required to color the regions of any map so that no two adjacent regions have the same color; that is, no two regions share a common boundary.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGrossTucker2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid6HmA&#95;x0dL9oCpgPA215&#95;215&#93;&#95;26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrossTucker2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid6HmA_x0dL9oCpgPA215_215]-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This theorem is stronger than <a href="/wiki/Five-color_theorem" class="mw-redirect" title="Five-color theorem">five-color theorem</a>. Relatedly, <a href="/wiki/Earth%E2%80%93Moon_problem" title="Earth–Moon problem">Earth–Moon problem</a> is known for the nowadays open problem on extension of the planar map coloring problem, solved by the four-color theorem.
</p><p>A voltage graph is a directed graph whose edges are labelled invertibly by elements of a <a href="/wiki/Group_(mathematics)" title="Group (mathematics)">group</a>. This graph concisely specifies the <a href="/wiki/Derived_graph" class="mw-redirect" title="Derived graph">derived graph</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGrossTucker2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid6HmA&#95;x0dL9oCpgPA57&#95;57&#93;&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrossTucker2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid6HmA_x0dL9oCpgPA57_57]-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is also a common way to form a <a href="/wiki/Covering_graph" title="Covering graph">covering graph</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGrossTucker2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid6HmA&#95;x0dL9oCpgPA72&#95;72&#93;&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrossTucker2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid6HmA_x0dL9oCpgPA72_72]-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Algebraic_graph_theory">Algebraic graph theory</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Graph_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Algebraic graph theory"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Algebraic_graph_theory" title="Algebraic graph theory">Algebraic graph theory</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Spectral_graph_theory" title="Spectral graph theory">Spectral graph theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Category:Topological_graph_theory" title="Category:Topological graph theory">Category:Topological graph theory</a></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Petersen1_tiny.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Petersen1_tiny.svg/250px-Petersen1_tiny.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="200" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Petersen1_tiny.svg/500px-Petersen1_tiny.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="220" data-file-height="220" /></a><figcaption>Algebraic graph theory uses group theory to study the symmetry of a graph. For example, <a href="/wiki/Petersen_graph" title="Petersen graph">Petersen graph</a> is high-symmetrical, known for <a href="/wiki/Vertex-transitive_graph" title="Vertex-transitive graph">vertex-transitive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Symmetric_graph" title="Symmetric graph">symmetric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Distance-transitive_graph" title="Distance-transitive graph">distance-transitive</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Distance-regular_graph" title="Distance-regular graph">distance-regular</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/Graph_automorphism" title="Graph automorphism">automorphism group</a> has 120 elements and <a href="/wiki/Symmetric_group" title="Symmetric group">symmetric group</a> <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle S_{5}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <msub>
          <mi>S</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>5</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle S_{5}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7d113353a42f71fc5e7154ddef2257079ab2e25a" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.479ex; height:2.509ex;" alt="{\displaystyle S_{5}}"></span>.</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Algebraic_graph_theory" title="Algebraic graph theory">Algebraic graph theory</a> is the study of graph theory that involves major branches of <a href="/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">algebra</a>. Major branches of algebra that are used are <a href="/wiki/Linear_algebra" title="Linear algebra">linear algebra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Group_theory" title="Group theory">group theory</a>.
</p><p>A study of graph theory using linear algebra is called <a href="/wiki/Spectral_graph_theory" title="Spectral graph theory">spectral graph theory</a>. This study focuses on <a href="/wiki/Adjacency_matrix" title="Adjacency matrix">adjacency matrix</a>, a matrix that represents the graph, and its <a href="/wiki/Eigendecomposition_of_a_matrix" title="Eigendecomposition of a matrix">spectrum</a>, which focuses on the <a href="/wiki/Characteristic_polynomial" title="Characteristic polynomial">characteristic polynomial</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eigenvalues_and_eigenvectors" title="Eigenvalues and eigenvectors">eigenvalues, and eigenvectors</a> of the given adjacency matrix. It also focuses on the <a href="/wiki/Laplacian_matrix" title="Laplacian matrix">Laplacian matrix</a> of a graph, which involves the <a href="/wiki/Degree_matrix" title="Degree matrix">degree matrix</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Diagonal_matrix" title="Diagonal matrix">diagonal matrix</a> that represents the degree of a vertex) and the adjacency matrix.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Group theory, particularly <a href="/wiki/Automorphism_group" title="Automorphism group">automorphism groups</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geometric_group_theory" title="Geometric group theory">geometric group theory</a>, focuses on various families of graphs based on symmetry in algebraic graph theory.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBiggs1993&#91;httpbooksgooglecombooksid6TasRmIFOxQCpgPA115&#95;Chapter&#95;15:&#95;Automorphisms&#95;of&#95;graphs&#93;&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiggs1993[httpbooksgooglecombooksid6TasRmIFOxQCpgPA115_Chapter_15:_Automorphisms_of_graphs]-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Such a symmetry includes <a href="/wiki/Symmetric_graph" title="Symmetric graph">symmetric graphs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vertex-transitive_graph" title="Vertex-transitive graph">vertex-transitive graphs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edge-transitive_graph" title="Edge-transitive graph">edge-transitive graphs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Distance-transitive_graph" title="Distance-transitive graph">distance-transitive graphs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Distance-regular_graph" title="Distance-regular graph">distance-regular graphs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Strongly_regular_graph" title="Strongly regular graph">strongly regular graphs</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGodsilRoyle2001&#91;httpbooksgooglecombooksidGeSPBAAAQBAJpgPR12&#95;xii–ixi&#93;&#95;31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGodsilRoyle2001[httpbooksgooglecombooksidGeSPBAAAQBAJpgPR12_xii–ixi]-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frucht%27s_theorem" title="Frucht&#39;s theorem">Frucht's theorem</a> states that every <a href="/wiki/Finite_group" title="Finite group">finite group</a> is the group of symmetries of a finite undirected graph, or more strongly, there exist infinitely many non-isomorphic simple connected graphs such that the automorphism group of each of them is isomorphic to a finite group.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGrossTucker2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid6HmA&#95;x0dL9oCpgPA70&#95;70&#93;&#95;32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrossTucker2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid6HmA_x0dL9oCpgPA70_70]-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Algebraic graph theory also studies the algebraic <a href="/wiki/Graph_invariant" class="mw-redirect" title="Graph invariant">invariants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chromatic_polynomial" title="Chromatic polynomial">chromatic polynomial</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tutte_polynomial" title="Tutte polynomial">Tutte polynomial</a> of a graph, and <a href="/wiki/Knot_invariant" title="Knot invariant">knot invariant</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGodsilRoyle2001&#91;httpbooksgooglecombooksidGeSPBAAAQBAJpgPR12&#95;xii–ixi&#93;&#95;31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGodsilRoyle2001[httpbooksgooglecombooksidGeSPBAAAQBAJpgPR12_xii–ixi]-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A graph invariant is a property of graphs that depends only on the abstract structure, instead of labellings or drawings of the graph. A chromatic polynomial is a polynomial that counts the number of graph colorings as a function of the number of colors.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBiggs1993&#91;httpbooksgooglecombooksid6TasRmIFOxQCpgPA64&#95;64&#93;&#95;33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiggs1993[httpbooksgooglecombooksid6TasRmIFOxQCpgPA64_64]-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Tutte polynomial is a two-variable polynomial on graph connectivity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBiggs1993&#91;httpbooksgooglecombooksid6TasRmIFOxQCpgPA98&#95;98&#93;&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiggs1993[httpbooksgooglecombooksid6TasRmIFOxQCpgPA98_98]-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Geometric_graph_theory">Geometric graph theory</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Graph_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Geometric graph theory"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geometric_graph_theory" title="Geometric graph theory">Geometric graph theory</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Graph_drawing" title="Graph drawing">Graph drawing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Category:Geometric_graph_theory" title="Category:Geometric graph theory">Category:Geometric graph theory</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1273380762/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" /><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:412px;max-width:412px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:201px;overflow:hidden"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Euclidean_minimum_spanning_tree.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Euclidean_minimum_spanning_tree.svg/250px-Euclidean_minimum_spanning_tree.svg.png" decoding="async" width="202" height="201" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Euclidean_minimum_spanning_tree.svg/500px-Euclidean_minimum_spanning_tree.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="270" data-file-height="269" /></a></span></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:201px;overflow:hidden"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Dodecahedron_schlegel.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Dodecahedron_schlegel.svg/250px-Dodecahedron_schlegel.svg.png" decoding="async" width="202" height="202" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Dodecahedron_schlegel.svg/500px-Dodecahedron_schlegel.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512" /></a></span></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:201px;max-width:201px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:188px;overflow:hidden"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Circle_packing_graph.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Circle_packing_graph.svg/250px-Circle_packing_graph.svg.png" decoding="async" width="199" height="189" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Circle_packing_graph.svg/500px-Circle_packing_graph.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="259" data-file-height="246" /></a></span></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:207px;max-width:207px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:188px;overflow:hidden"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Fano_plane-Levi_graph.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Fano_plane-Levi_graph.svg/250px-Fano_plane-Levi_graph.svg.png" decoding="async" width="205" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Fano_plane-Levi_graph.svg/500px-Fano_plane-Levi_graph.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="992" data-file-height="911" /></a></span></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Geometric graph theory studies a graph drawn with straight-line or continuous curved edges with geometric properties combinatorially. Within these illustrations, the geometric graph theory studies:
<div><ul><li>the <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_graph" class="mw-redirect" title="Euclidean graph">Euclidean graph</a> as in <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_minimum_spanning_tree" title="Euclidean minimum spanning tree">Euclidean minimum spanning tree</a> (top left);</li><li><a href="/wiki/Polyhedral_graph" title="Polyhedral graph">polyhedral graph</a> with an example of <a href="/wiki/Dodecahedral_graph" class="mw-redirect" title="Dodecahedral graph">dodecahedral graph</a> for a regular dodecahedron (top right);</li><li>the <a href="/wiki/Intersection_graph" title="Intersection graph">intersection graph</a> of geometric sets like the <a href="/wiki/Coin_graph" class="mw-redirect" title="Coin graph">coin graph</a> with <a href="/wiki/Circle_packing" title="Circle packing">circle packing</a> (bottom left); and</li><li>the <a href="/wiki/Incidence_geometry" title="Incidence geometry">incidence geometry</a> as in the <a href="/wiki/Heawood_graph" title="Heawood graph">Heawood graph</a> being the <a href="/wiki/Levi_graph" title="Levi graph">Levi graph</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fano_plane" title="Fano plane">Fano plane</a> (bottom right).</li></ul></div></div></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Geometric_graph_theory" title="Geometric graph theory">Geometric graph theory</a> focuses on combinatorial and geometric properties of a graph that is drawn in a plane with straight-line or continuous curved edges in <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_space" title="Euclidean space">Euclidean space</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPach2018&#91;httpbooksgooglecombooksid9mlQDwAAQBAJpgPA257&#95;257&#93;&#95;35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPach2018[httpbooksgooglecombooksid9mlQDwAAQBAJpgPA257_257]-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As part of <a href="/wiki/Discrete_geometry" title="Discrete geometry">discrete geometry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computational_geometry" title="Computational geometry">computational geometry</a>, geometric graph theory studies <a href="/wiki/Planar_graph" title="Planar graph">planar graphs</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBounceurBezouiEuler2019&#91;httpbooksgooglecombooksidUmYIEQAAQBAJpgPA13&#95;13–14&#93;&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBounceurBezouiEuler2019[httpbooksgooglecombooksidUmYIEQAAQBAJpgPA13_13–14]-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> relationship to <a href="/wiki/Polytope" title="Polytope">higher-dimensional convex polytopes</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Intersection" title="Intersection">intersection</a> of geometrical shaped sets,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMcKeeMcMorris1999&#91;httpbooksgooglecombooksidphWjyYLP7d4CpgPA1&#95;1&amp;ndash;2&#93;&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKeeMcMorris1999[httpbooksgooglecombooksidphWjyYLP7d4CpgPA1_1&amp;ndash;2]-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and other geometries' subareas of <a href="/wiki/Incidence_geometry" title="Incidence geometry">incidence geometry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Projective_geometry" title="Projective geometry">projective geometry</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGrünbaum2006&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidcKpBGcqpspICpgPA181&#95;181&#93;&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrünbaum2006[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidcKpBGcqpspICpgPA181_181]-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A planar graph wherein its vertices are embedded as points, and its edges are non-crossing <a href="/wiki/Line_segment" title="Line segment">line segments</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_plane" title="Euclidean plane">Euclidean plane</a> is called <a href="/wiki/Planar_straight-line_graph" title="Planar straight-line graph">planar straight-line graph</a>. Any planar graph can be represented as a planar straight-line graph by <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A1ry%27s_theorem" title="Fáry&#39;s theorem">Fáry's theorem</a>. The planar straight-line graph is the special case of a Euclidean graph. The Euclidean graph allows its edges to have the length of the <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_distance" title="Euclidean distance">Euclidean distance</a> between its endpoints. Its notions are the <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_minimum_spanning_tree" title="Euclidean minimum spanning tree">Euclidean minimum spanning tree</a> on minimizing the total length of the segments for finite points in any <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_space" title="Euclidean space">Euclidean space</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hadwiger%E2%80%93Nelson_problem" title="Hadwiger–Nelson problem">Hadwiger–Nelson problem</a> on asking for the minimum number of coloring plane such that no two points at a <a href="/wiki/Unit_distance_graph" title="Unit distance graph">unit distance</a> from each other have the same color, and <a href="/wiki/Shortest_path_problem" title="Shortest path problem">shortest path problem</a> on finding a path between two vertices in a graph that minimizes the sum of the assigned values of its edges.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBounceurBezouiEuler2019&#91;httpbooksgooglecombooksidUmYIEQAAQBAJpgPA13&#95;13–14&#93;&#95;36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBounceurBezouiEuler2019[httpbooksgooglecombooksidUmYIEQAAQBAJpgPA13_13–14]-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Visibility_graph" title="Visibility graph">visibility graph</a> is a graph whose vertices and edges are the point locations and visible connections, respectively. In a <a href="/wiki/Simple_polygon" title="Simple polygon">simple polygon</a>, where its edges are not self-intersecting and have no holes, the vertices of a visibility graph are connected by edges that represent the sides and diagonals of a polygon. The vertices are defined as the point locations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEEverettCorneil1995&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEverettCorneil1995-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Polyhedral_graph" title="Polyhedral graph">Polyhedral graph</a> is an undirected graph that forms the vertices and edges of a three-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Convex_polyhedron" class="mw-redirect" title="Convex polyhedron">convex polyhedron</a>. In order to achieve it, such a graph must meet the requirements of <a href="/wiki/Steinitz%27s_theorem" title="Steinitz&#39;s theorem">Steinitz's theorem</a>, stating that every convex polyhedron is <a href="/wiki/Vertex_connectivity" title="Vertex connectivity">3-vertex connected planar graph</a>. The planar graph remains connected whenever any two of its vertices are removed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEZiegler2007628–642&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZiegler2007628–642-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/Intersection_graph" title="Intersection graph">intersection graph</a> is a graph in which each vertex is associated with a set and in which vertices are connected by edges whenever the corresponding sets have a nonempty <a href="/wiki/Intersection" title="Intersection">intersection</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMcKeeMcMorris1999&#91;httpbooksgooglecombooksidphWjyYLP7d4CpgPA1&#95;1&amp;ndash;2&#93;&#95;38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKeeMcMorris1999[httpbooksgooglecombooksidphWjyYLP7d4CpgPA1_1&amp;ndash;2]-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Each vertex is represented as a set, and every two vertices are connected. Hence, the intersection graph of finite sets can be represented through the smallest number of required elements, known as the <a href="/wiki/Intersection_number_(graph_theory)" title="Intersection number (graph theory)">intersection number</a>. The result graph can be geometric whenever the sets are geometrical objects. For instance, the intersection graph of line segments in one dimension is an <a href="/wiki/Interval_graph" title="Interval graph">interval graph</a>. The intersection graph of unit disks in the plane is a <a href="/wiki/Unit_disk_graph" title="Unit disk graph">unit disk graph</a>. The intersection of a <a href="/wiki/Circle_packing" title="Circle packing">circle packing</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Coin_graph" class="mw-redirect" title="Coin graph">coin graph</a>, where a vertex and an edge represent a circle and every pair of tangent circles; by Koebe–Andreev–Thurston theorem, the intersection graphs of non-crossing circles are exactly the planar graphs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Scheinerman%27s_conjecture" title="Scheinerman&#39;s conjecture">Scheinerman's theorem</a> states that every planar graph can be represented as the intersection graph of line segments in the plane.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEChalopinGonçalves2009&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChalopinGonçalves2009-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Levi_graph" title="Levi graph">Levi graph</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Bipartite_graph" title="Bipartite graph">bipartite graph</a> that associates to the <a href="/wiki/Incidence_structure" title="Incidence structure">incidence structure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Configuration_(geometry)" title="Configuration (geometry)">projective configuration</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEGrünbaum2006&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidcKpBGcqpspICpgPA181&#95;181&#93;&#95;39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrünbaum2006[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidcKpBGcqpspICpgPA181_181]-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>By applying to <a href="/wiki/Information_visualization" class="mw-redirect" title="Information visualization">information visualization</a>, this creates another subarea of graph theory that is known as <a href="/wiki/Graph_drawing" title="Graph drawing">graph drawing</a>, which visualizes a graph depiction. Frequently drawn as node–link diagrams, the vertices of a graph are represented as disks, boxes, or textual labels, and the edges are represented as line segments, polylines, or curves in the Euclidean plane.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEDi&#95;BattistaEadesTamassiaTollis1994viii&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDi_BattistaEadesTamassiaTollis1994viii-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many definitions for graph drawings based on quality measures include the crossing number,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEDi&#95;BattistaEadesTamassiaTollis199414&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDi_BattistaEadesTamassiaTollis199414-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Area_(graph_drawing)" title="Area (graph drawing)">area</a>, symmetry display on finding the problem of a graph's group automorphism,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEDi&#95;BattistaEadesTamassiaTollis199416&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDi_BattistaEadesTamassiaTollis199416-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bend_minimization" title="Bend minimization">bend minimization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angular_resolution_(graph_drawing)" title="Angular resolution (graph drawing)">angular resolution</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPachSharir2009&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPachSharir2009-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Slope_number" title="Slope number">slope number</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPachSharir2009&#95;47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPachSharir2009-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Tools for graph drawings are the circle packing,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMalitzPapakostas1994&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalitzPapakostas1994-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the intersection graph, and other visualizations of the adjacency matrix.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Extremal_graph_theory">Extremal graph theory</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Graph_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Extremal graph theory"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Extremal_graph_theory" title="Extremal graph theory">Extremal graph theory</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Extremal_graph_theory" title="Category:Extremal graph theory">Category:Extremal graph theory</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Biclique_K_5_5.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Biclique_K_5_5.svg/250px-Biclique_K_5_5.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="123" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Biclique_K_5_5.svg/500px-Biclique_K_5_5.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="163" data-file-height="80" /></a><figcaption>Extremal graph theory studies the maximum number of a graph's edges, known as the extremal number. The origin is from Mantel's theorem on finding the extremal number of a <a href="/wiki/Triangle-free_graph" title="Triangle-free graph">triangle-free graph</a> (illustrated), which is <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle \lfloor n^{2}/4\rfloor }">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#x230A;<!-- ⌊ --></mo>
        <msup>
          <mi>n</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mn>2</mn>
          </mrow>
        </msup>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mo>/</mo>
        </mrow>
        <mn>4</mn>
        <mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#x230B;<!-- ⌋ --></mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \lfloor n^{2}/4\rfloor }</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c9ee46de44c6ef9a36d80e4119114dbe14cb7d1f" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.839ex; height:3.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle \lfloor n^{2}/4\rfloor }"></span>.</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Extremal_graph_theory" title="Extremal graph theory">Extremal graph theory</a> is a branch of mathematics at the intersection of <a href="/wiki/Extremal_combinatorics" title="Extremal combinatorics">extremal combinatorics</a> and graph theory. This area studies the maximum number of a graph's edges, known as the extremal number.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEChartrandJordonVatterZhang2024&#91;httpbooksgooglecombooksid01cIEQAAQBAJpgPA221&#95;221&#93;&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChartrandJordonVatterZhang2024[httpbooksgooglecombooksid01cIEQAAQBAJpgPA221_221]-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The subarea's milestone originated from Mantel's theorem on the extremal number of a <a href="/wiki/Triangle-free_graph" title="Triangle-free graph">triangle-free graph</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tur%C3%A1n%27s_theorem" title="Turán&#39;s theorem">Turán's theorem</a> extended Mantel's theorem for any undirected graph that does not have a complete subgraph of a given size. Turán's theorem is generalized by <a href="/wiki/Erd%C5%91s%E2%80%93Stone_theorem" title="Erdős–Stone theorem">Erdős–Stone theorem</a>, which is occasionally known as the "fundamental theorem of extremal graph theory".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBollobas2013&#91;httpbooksgooglecombooksidJeIlBQAAQBAJpgPA104&#95;104&#93;&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBollobas2013[httpbooksgooglecombooksidJeIlBQAAQBAJpgPA104_104]-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Extremal graph theory also studies <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_subgraph_problem" title="Forbidden subgraph problem">forbidden subgraph problem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Homomorphism_density" title="Homomorphism density">homomorphism density</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Szemer%C3%A9di_regularity_lemma" title="Szemerédi regularity lemma">Szemerédi regularity lemma</a>. The forbidden subgraphs problem suggests finding the extremal number of a graph with <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle n}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>n</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle n}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.395ex; height:1.676ex;" alt="{\displaystyle n}"></span> vertices such that it does not have a subgraph that is <a href="/wiki/Graph_isomorphism" title="Graph isomorphism">isomorphic</a> to the graph.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBollobas2013&#91;httpbooksgooglecombooksidJeIlBQAAQBAJpgPA123&#95;123&#93;&#95;51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBollobas2013[httpbooksgooglecombooksidJeIlBQAAQBAJpgPA123_123]-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Homomorphism_density" title="Homomorphism density">homomorphism density</a> is a parameter that involves the <a href="/wiki/Graph_homomorphism" title="Graph homomorphism">graph homomorphism</a>. The density may refer to the probability that a map from the vertices of a graph to the vertices of another one chosen uniformly at random is a graph homomorphism. Being homomorphic means there exists a mapping between two graphs that respects their structure, or equivalently, a function between the vertex sets of two graphs that maps adjacent vertices to adjacent vertices.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Szemerédi's regularity lemma states that a graph can be partitioned into a bounded number of parts so that the edges between parts are <span class="mwe-math-element mwe-math-element-inline"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle \varepsilon }">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>&#x03B5;<!-- ε --></mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \varepsilon }</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a30c89172e5b88edbd45d3e2772c7f5e562e5173" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline mw-invert skin-invert" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.083ex; height:1.676ex;" alt="{\displaystyle \varepsilon }"></span>-regular.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Theory_of_random_graph">Theory of random graph</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Graph_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Theory of random graph"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Random_graph" title="Random graph">Random graph</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Random_graphs" title="Category:Random graphs">Category:Random graphs</a></div>
<p>The theory of <a href="/wiki/Random_graph" title="Random graph">random graph</a> focuses on graphs using <a href="/wiki/Probabilistic_method" title="Probabilistic method">probabilistic method</a>. The subarea was founded by Hungarian mathematicians <a href="/wiki/Paul_Erd%C5%91s" title="Paul Erdős">Paul Erdős</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alfr%C3%A9d_R%C3%A9nyi" title="Alfréd Rényi">Alfréd Rényi</a>, whose modelling generates random graphs, known as <a href="/wiki/Erd%C5%91s%E2%80%93R%C3%A9nyi_model" title="Erdős–Rényi model">Erdős–Rényi model</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEBollobás2001&#91;httpbooksgooglecombooksido9WecWgilzYCpgPR10&#95;xi&#93;&#95;18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBollobás2001[httpbooksgooglecombooksido9WecWgilzYCpgPR10_xi]-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Random_minimum_spanning_tree.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Random_minimum_spanning_tree.svg/250px-Random_minimum_spanning_tree.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="158" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Random_minimum_spanning_tree.svg/500px-Random_minimum_spanning_tree.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="259" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Random_minimum_spanning_tree" title="Random minimum spanning tree">Random minimum spanning tree</a> on the same graph but with randomized weights</figcaption></figure>
<p>This subarea studies the <a href="/wiki/Random_tree" title="Random tree">random tree</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Tree_(graph_theory)" title="Tree (graph theory)">tree</a> is an undirected graph where every pair of vertices is connected by exactly one <a href="/wiki/Path_(graph_theory)" title="Path (graph theory)">path</a>. Therefore, a random tree is a tree that is formed by a <a href="/wiki/Stochastic_process" title="Stochastic process">stochastic process</a>. Many types of random trees include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Uniform_spanning_tree" class="mw-redirect" title="Uniform spanning tree">Uniform spanning tree</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Spanning_tree" title="Spanning tree">spanning tree</a> of a given graph in which each different tree is <a href="/wiki/Discrete_uniform_distribution" title="Discrete uniform distribution">equally likely to be selected</a>. Being a spanning tree means that a subgraph is a tree that includes all of the vertices of a graph. The uniform spanning tree can be generated by using the method of random simple path known as the <a href="/wiki/Loop-erased_random_walk" title="Loop-erased random walk">loop-erased random walk</a>, of taking a random walk on the given graph and erasing the cycles created by this walk.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEWilson1996&#95;53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson1996-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Branching_process" title="Branching process">Branching process</a>, a model of a population in which each individual has a random number of children</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brownian_tree" title="Brownian tree">Brownian tree</a>, a fractal tree structure created by diffusion-limited aggregation processes</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Random_binary_tree" title="Random binary tree">Random binary tree</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Binary_tree" title="Binary tree">binary tree</a> selected at random from some <a href="/wiki/Probability_distribution" title="Probability distribution">probability distribution</a> on binary trees.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTESedgewickFlajolet2013286&#95;54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESedgewickFlajolet2013286-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This includes trees formed by random insertion orders,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMorin2004&#95;55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorin2004-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and trees that are uniformly distributed with a given number of nodes.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Random_forest" title="Random forest">Random forest</a>, a machine-learning classifier based on choosing random subsets of variables for each tree and using the most frequent tree output as the overall classification.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Random_minimal_spanning_tree" class="mw-redirect" title="Random minimal spanning tree">Random minimal spanning tree</a> is a spanning tree of a graph formed by choosing random edge weights and using the <a href="/wiki/Minimum_spanning_tree" title="Minimum spanning tree">minimum spanning tree</a> for those weights.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEMcDiarmidJohnsonStone1997&#95;56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcDiarmidJohnsonStone1997-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Random_recursive_tree" title="Random recursive tree">Random recursive tree</a>, increasingly labelled trees, which can be generated using a simple stochastic growth rule.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rapidly_exploring_random_tree" title="Rapidly exploring random tree">Rapidly exploring random tree</a>, a fractal space-filling pattern used as a data structure for searching high-dimensional spaces.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Treap" title="Treap">Treap</a> or randomized binary search tree, a data structure that uses random choices to simulate a random binary tree for non-random update sequences</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Graph_enumeration">Graph enumeration</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Graph_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Graph enumeration"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Graph_enumeration" title="Graph enumeration">Graph enumeration</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Applications">Applications</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Graph_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Applications"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Wikipedia_multilingual_network_graph_July_2013.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Wikipedia_multilingual_network_graph_July_2013.svg/250px-Wikipedia_multilingual_network_graph_July_2013.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="229" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Wikipedia_multilingual_network_graph_July_2013.svg/500px-Wikipedia_multilingual_network_graph_July_2013.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="918" data-file-height="841" /></a><figcaption>The network graph formed by Wikipedia editors (edges) contributing to different Wikipedia language versions (vertices) during one month in summer 2013<sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>Graphs can be used to model many types of relations and processes in physical, biological,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> social and information systems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many practical problems can be represented by graphs. Emphasizing their application to real-world systems, the term <i>network</i> is sometimes defined to mean a graph in which attributes (e.g. names) are associated with the vertices and edges, and the subject that expresses and understands real-world systems as a network is called <a href="/wiki/Network_science" title="Network science">network science</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Computer_science">Computer science</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Graph_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Computer science"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Within <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>, '<a href="/wiki/Cybernetics" title="Cybernetics">causal</a>' and 'non-causal' linked structures are graphs that are used to represent networks of communication, data organization, computational devices, the flow of computation, etc. For instance, the link structure of a <a href="/wiki/Website" title="Website">website</a> can be represented by a directed graph, in which the vertices (nodes) represent web pages and directed edges represent <a href="/wiki/Hyperlink" title="Hyperlink">links</a> from one page to another. A similar approach can be taken to problems in social media,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> travel, biology, computer chip design, mapping the progression of neuro-degenerative diseases,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and many other fields. The development of <a href="/wiki/Algorithm" title="Algorithm">algorithms</a> to <a href="/wiki/List_of_algorithms#Graph_algorithms" title="List of algorithms">handle graphs</a> is therefore of major interest in computer science. The <a href="/wiki/Graph_transformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Graph transformation">transformation of graphs</a> is often formalized and represented by <a href="/wiki/Graph_rewriting" title="Graph rewriting">graph rewrite systems</a>. Complementary to <a href="/wiki/Graph_transformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Graph transformation">graph transformation</a> systems focusing on rule-based in-memory manipulation of graphs are <a href="/wiki/Graph_database" title="Graph database">graph databases</a> geared towards <a href="/wiki/Database_transaction" title="Database transaction">transaction</a>-safe, <a href="/wiki/Persistence_(computer_science)" title="Persistence (computer science)">persistent</a> storing and querying of <a href="/wiki/Graph_(data_structure)" class="mw-redirect" title="Graph (data structure)">graph-structured data</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Linguistics">Linguistics</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Graph_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Linguistics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Graph-theoretic methods, in various forms, have proven particularly useful in <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>, since natural language often lends itself well to discrete structure. Traditionally, <a href="/wiki/Syntax" title="Syntax">syntax</a> and compositional semantics follow tree-based structures, whose expressive power lies in the <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_compositionality" title="Principle of compositionality">principle of compositionality</a>, modeled in a hierarchical graph. More contemporary approaches such as <a href="/wiki/Head-driven_phrase_structure_grammar" title="Head-driven phrase structure grammar">head-driven phrase structure grammar</a> model the syntax of natural language using <a href="/wiki/Feature_structure" title="Feature structure">typed feature structures</a>, which are <a href="/wiki/Directed_acyclic_graph" title="Directed acyclic graph">directed acyclic graphs</a>. 
Within <a href="/wiki/Lexical_semantics" title="Lexical semantics">lexical semantics</a>, especially as applied to computers, modeling word meaning is easier when a given word is understood in terms of related words; <a href="/wiki/Semantic_network" title="Semantic network">semantic networks</a> are therefore important in <a href="/wiki/Computational_linguistics" title="Computational linguistics">computational linguistics</a>.
